3:T11a3,# Prune Stale WIP Revisions Using Revsets

## Background

You are an open-source maintainer working on a project called `mylib` hosted on GitHub. You use Jujutsu (`jj`) as your version control system over a Git-backed repository.

Over the past few days you have been experimenting locally and left behind several scratch/WIP (work-in-progress) revisions that were never promoted to a proper bookmark or given a meaningful description. You now want to clean up the repository by **abandoning** those stale WIP revisions before pushing your real work upstream.

The repository already has the following history set up for you:

```
 root()
  └── "Initial project scaffold"         ← bookmark: main
       └── "Add core library module"      ← bookmark: feature/core
            ├── "(no description set)"    ← WIP scratch commit A (no bookmark)
            └── "(no description set)"    ← WIP scratch commit B (no bookmark)
```

Both WIP commits are:
- Direct children of the `feature/core` bookmark commit
- Empty of any useful description (description is blank / empty string)
- Not pointed to by any local bookmark

Your task is to **abandon all mutable revisions that have an empty description AND are not ancestors of any local bookmark**, then verify the result.

## Starting Environment

- Working directory: `/home/user/mylib`
- The repository is already initialised as a `jj` git-backed repo
- User identity is already configured in jj config
- The commit history described above is already in place
- `jj` binary is available in `PATH`

## Your Task

Complete the following steps **inside `/home/user/mylib`**:

### Step 1 — Inspect the current log

Run `jj log` to see the current history and identify the WIP revisions:

```bash
cd /home/user/mylib
jj log
```

You should see at least four revisions: the root, `main`, `feature/core`, and two WIP commits that have no description and no bookmark.

### Step 2 — Abandon all WIP revisions using a revset

Use a single `jj abandon` command with a revset expression to abandon **all mutable revisions that have an empty description and are not ancestors of any local bookmark**.

The revset you need is:

```
mutable() & empty_description & ~::bookmarks()
```

where `empty_description` is expressed using the `description("")` function (which matches commits whose description is the empty string).

The exact command to run:

```bash
jj abandon 'mutable() & description("") & ~::bookmarks()'
```

This revset reads as:
- `mutable()` — only commits that are not immutable (i.e., not protected remote/tag heads)
- `description("")` — only commits whose description is the empty string
- `~::bookmarks()` — exclude commits that are ancestors-of (or equal to) any local bookmark target

**Important**: The `~::bookmarks()` part uses the `::` range operator to compute the set of all ancestors of bookmark targets (inclusive), and then negates it with `~`. This ensures you only abandon commits that are genuinely not reachable from any named bookmark.

### Step 3 — Verify the result

After abandoning, confirm the cleanup was successful:

```bash
jj log
```

You should now see **only** the revisions that have bookmarks (`main`, `feature/core`) and the working-copy commit. The two WIP scratch commits must no longer appear in the visible history.

Also verify with a revset query:

```bash
jj log -r 'mutable() & description("") & ~::bookmarks()'
```

This should output **nothing** (or an empty result), confirming all matching WIP revisions have been abandoned.

## Expected Final State

- The two WIP revisions (empty description, no bookmark) are **abandoned** (no longer visible)
- The `main` bookmark still points to the "Initial project scaffold" commit
- The `feature/core` bookmark still points to the "Add core library module" commit
- `jj log -r 'mutable() & description("") & ~::bookmarks()'` returns an empty result
- The file `/home/user/mylib/README.md` still exists (it was part of the scaffold)
- The file `/home/user/mylib/src/lib.rs` still exists (it was part of the core module)

## Verification Conditions

Automated tests will check:
1. `jj log -r 'mutable() & description("") & ~::bookmarks()'` in `/home/user/mylib` returns an empty result (exit 0 but no commits shown, or the message indicating no revisions)
2. `jj bookmark list` shows both `main` and `feature/core` bookmarks still present
3. The file `/home/user/mylib/README.md` exists
4. The file `/home/user/mylib/src/lib.rs` exists
4:T1331,# Create and Move Bookmarks in a jj Repository

## Context

You are a developer who has recently migrated from Git to Jujutsu (`jj`). You are working on a small web application stored in a `jj` repository at `/home/user/webapp`. In Jujutsu, **bookmarks** serve the same role as Git branches — they are named pointers to specific commits. Unlike Git, Jujutsu does not have an "active" branch concept; bookmarks are simply labels that you move around manually or via specific commands.

The repository currently has the following commit history (oldest to newest):

1. **`chore: init webapp`** — the root commit, adds `README.md` and `app/__init__.py`
2. **`feat: add homepage route`** — introduces `app/routes.py` with a basic homepage handler
3. **`feat: add contact page`** — introduces `app/contact.py` with a contact page handler

Commits 2 and 3 are stacked linearly above the root. The working copy (`@`) is currently an empty commit sitting on top of commit 3 (i.e., `@-` is `feat: add contact page`).

There is currently **one bookmark** in the repository:

- `dev` — pointing to commit 2 (`feat: add homepage route`)

There is **no bookmark** on commit 3 yet.

## Your Assignment

You need to organize the bookmarks before pushing this work for code review. Complete the following **two tasks** using only `jj` commands:

### Task 1 — Create a new bookmark `feature/contact` on commit 3

Create a bookmark named `feature/contact` that points to commit 3 — the commit with description `feat: add contact page`.

In jj, commit 3 is the **parent of the working copy**, which can be referenced with the revset `@-`. Use the `jj bookmark create` command with the `-r` option to specify the target revision:

```
jj bookmark create <bookmark-name> -r <revision>
```

The bookmark name must be exactly `feature/contact` (with a forward slash). You do not need to use `sudo`, and you do not need to modify any files — only bookmark management commands are required.

After creating this bookmark, verify it exists by running `jj bookmark list`. You should see `feature/contact` pointing to the commit with description `feat: add contact page`.

### Task 2 — Move the existing `dev` bookmark forward to commit 3

The `dev` bookmark currently points to commit 2 (`feat: add homepage route`). You need to move it **forward** to also point to commit 3 (`feat: add contact page`, i.e., `@-`).

In jj, moving an existing bookmark is done with `jj bookmark move`:

```
jj bookmark move <bookmark-name> --to <revision>
```

Moving a bookmark **forward** (to a descendant) does not require any special flags. The `--allow-backwards` flag is only needed when moving a bookmark to an ancestor or unrelated commit. Since commit 3 is a descendant of commit 2, you can move `dev` directly:

```
jj bookmark move dev --to @-
```

> **Note for Git migrants:** In Git, you would use `git branch -f dev <commit>` to move an existing branch. In jj, `jj bookmark move` is the equivalent operation. Unlike Git's `git checkout -b`, `jj bookmark create` does not "switch" to the bookmark — bookmarks in jj are not "checked out". You can work on any commit regardless of bookmarks.

### Final Verification

After completing both tasks, use `jj bookmark list` to verify your work:

- `dev` should point to the commit with description `feat: add contact page`
- `feature/contact` should point to the commit with description `feat: add contact page`
- Both bookmarks should point to the **same commit** (commit 3, which is `@-`)

You can also run `jj log` to see the bookmarks displayed alongside their respective commits in the history graph.

## Starting Environment Assumptions

- Home directory: `/home/user`
- The `jj` repository is at `/home/user/webapp`
- `jj` is installed and available on `$PATH`
- `git` is installed (the repo uses the jj-git backend)
- User identity is pre-configured: `user.name = "Dev User"`, `user.email = "dev@example.com"`
- The commit history contains exactly three commits: the root (`chore: init webapp`), commit 2 (`feat: add homepage route`), and commit 3 (`feat: add contact page`)
- `app/__init__.py` exists in the repo (added in the root commit)
- `app/routes.py` exists in the repo (added in commit 2)
- `app/contact.py` exists in the repo (added in commit 3)
- A bookmark named `dev` already exists and points to commit 2 (`feat: add homepage route`)
- No other bookmarks exist at the start
- The working copy (`@`) is an empty commit whose parent is commit 3

## Important Notes

- Do **not** use `git` commands directly — all operations must use `jj`.
- Do **not** use `sudo`.
- The new bookmark must be named exactly `feature/contact` (with a forward slash).
- The moved bookmark must remain named `dev`.
- Both bookmarks must point to the commit with description `feat: add contact page` after you are done.
- You only need **2 jj commands** to complete the entire task.
5:Tfcf,# Create and Move Bookmarks as Release Manager

## Context

You are a release manager working on a software project stored in a `jj` (Jujutsu) repository at `/home/user/project`. Your team uses bookmarks to track topic branches that correspond to individual features being developed in parallel. Each feature lives in a linear stack of commits on top of the main trunk commit.

The repository currently contains the following commit history (from oldest to newest):

1. `chore: initialize project` — the trunk commit, contains `README.md`
2. `feat: implement user login` — introduces `src/login.py`
3. `feat: implement password reset` — introduces `src/reset.py`

Commit 2 and commit 3 form a linear stack above the trunk. The working copy (`@`) is an empty commit sitting on top of commit 3.

A bookmark named `feature/auth` currently exists and **points to commit 2** (`feat: implement user login`). There is no bookmark on commit 3 yet.

## Your Assignment

As release manager, you need to organize the topic branches before review. Complete the following **two tasks** using only `jj` commands:

### Task 1 — Create a new bookmark `feature/password-reset` on commit 3

Create a bookmark named `feature/password-reset` pointing to the commit with description `feat: implement password reset` (which is the parent of the working copy, i.e. `@-`).

Use `jj bookmark create` to create this bookmark. You can target the revision using the revset `@-` (parent of the working copy).

Example syntax:
```
jj bookmark create feature/password-reset -r @-
```

### Task 2 — Move bookmark `feature/auth` forward to commit 3

The `feature/auth` bookmark currently points to commit 2 (`feat: implement user login`). You need to move it **forward** to also point to commit 3 — i.e., `@-` (the parent of the working copy, which is `feat: implement password reset`).

Use `jj bookmark move` with the `--to` option and the `--allow-backwards` flag is **not** needed since you are moving forward. However, if you need to move to a specific target, use:

```
jj bookmark move feature/auth --to @-
```

> **Note:** `jj bookmark move` moves an *existing* bookmark to a new revision. Unlike `jj bookmark create`, it will error if the bookmark does not already exist. The `--allow-backwards` flag is only needed when moving a bookmark to an *older* revision.

### Verification

After completing both tasks, run `jj bookmark list` to confirm:

- A bookmark named `feature/auth` exists and points to the commit with description `feat: implement password reset` (commit 3, i.e. `@-`).
- A bookmark named `feature/password-reset` exists and also points to the commit with description `feat: implement password reset` (commit 3, i.e. `@-`).

Both bookmarks must point to the **same commit** — the one whose description starts with `feat: implement password reset`.

## Starting Environment Assumptions

- Home directory: `/home/user`
- The `jj` repository is at `/home/user/project`
- `jj` is installed and available on `$PATH`
- `git` is installed (the repo uses the jj-git backend)
- User identity is pre-configured: `user.name = "Dev User"`, `user.email = "dev@example.com"`
- There are exactly two feature commits above the trunk commit (`chore: initialize project`)
- Commit 2 is `feat: implement user login` and introduces `src/login.py`
- Commit 3 is `feat: implement password reset` and introduces `src/reset.py`
- A bookmark named `feature/auth` already exists pointing to commit 2
- No other bookmarks exist at the start
- The working copy (`@`) is an empty commit on top of commit 3

## Important Notes

- Do **not** use `git` commands directly. All operations must use `jj`.
- Do **not** use `sudo`.
- The new bookmark must be named exactly `feature/password-reset` (with a forward slash).
- The existing bookmark must be named exactly `feature/auth` (with a forward slash).
- Both bookmarks must point to the commit with description `feat: implement password reset` after you are done.
- You only need **2-3 jj commands** to complete this task.
6:Ta11,# Criss-Cross Merge Resolution

## Context

You are a reviewer working on a repository at `/home/user/repo`. The repository has a complex criss-cross merge history:

- `main-v1` is the original base commit
- `branch-a` started from `main-v1`, then at some point merged `branch-b` into itself
- `branch-b` started from `main-v1`, then at some point merged `branch-a` into itself

This creates a criss-cross pattern where both branches have each other in their history, making traditional merges ambiguous.

## Files Involved

- `src/core.py` — modified by both branches with conflicting changes
- `src/utils.py` — modified by both branches with conflicting changes
- `src/feature_a.py` — unique to branch-a
- `src/feature_b.py` — unique to branch-b

## Your Task

1. **Identify** the common ancestor of `branch-a` and `branch-b` (the `main-v1` commit). Use `jj log` to inspect the history.

2. **Create** a new working commit starting from `main-v1` using `jj new`.

3. **Cherry-pick** (using `jj restore --from` or `jj squash`) the unique changes from both branches in logical order:
   - First apply the unique file changes from `branch-a`
   - Then apply the unique file changes from `branch-b`

4. **Resolve conflicts** in `src/core.py` and `src/utils.py`:
   - In `src/core.py`: the final file must contain both the `initialize()` function from branch-a AND the `shutdown()` function from branch-b.
   - In `src/utils.py`: the final file must contain both the `format_output()` function from branch-a AND the `parse_input()` function from branch-b.

5. **Ensure** `src/feature_a.py` and `src/feature_b.py` are both present in the working copy.

6. **Create** an `integration` bookmark pointing to the current working copy commit:
   ```
   jj bookmark create integration -r @
   ```

7. **Write** `/home/user/criss_cross_log.txt` with exactly this format:
   ```
   branch_a_tip: <change_id_of_branch_a_tip>
   branch_b_tip: <change_id_of_branch_b_tip>
   integration_commit: <change_id_of_integration_commit>
   ```
   Use `jj log -r 'branch-a' --no-graph -T 'change_id'` to get the change IDs.

## Constraints

- Do NOT use `git` commands for history manipulation — use only `jj` commands
- The `integration` bookmark must point to a commit with no conflict markers
- Both `src/feature_a.py` and `src/feature_b.py` must exist in the working copy
- `src/core.py` must contain both `initialize()` and `shutdown()` functions
- `src/utils.py` must contain both `format_output()` and `parse_input()` functions
- The log file must use the exact key names shown above
7:T68a,You are a developer migrating from Git to Jujutsu (jj). A teammate has concurrently modified and pushed the same commit you were working on, resulting in a divergent commit in your local repository after you fetched from the remote.

Your task is to resolve this divergence.

Here is your environment:
- You are in a jj repository located at `/home/user/repo`.
- There is a bookmark `feature` which currently points to a divergent commit. One side of the divergence has your local changes (adding a feature in `main.py`), and the other side has the remote changes (a bugfix in `main.py`).

Perform the following steps:
1. Identify the divergent commits using `jj log`.
2. Resolve the divergence. Since the commits are divergent, they share the same change ID. You need to resolve them into a single commit with that change ID. You can do this by abandoning one side and keeping the other, or by rebasing/squashing them. However, the standard way to resolve divergence in jj is to rebase one side onto the other or squash them if they represent the same logical change. Actually, `jj squash` or `jj rebase` can handle it, but wait, divergent commits have the SAME change ID. If you just edit the file and `jj squash` or `jj new`, you might still have divergence. Wait, if you `jj squash -r <side1> --into <side2>`, it resolves the divergence. Let's ask the user to resolve it such that the `feature` bookmark points to a single commit with no divergence, containing both changes.
3. Write the change ID (not commit ID) of the resolved commit to `/home/user/resolved_change_id.txt`.
4. Write the content of `main.py` from the resolved commit to `/home/user/main_content.txt`.
8:Tbdb,You are a contributor who uses jj and needs to submit a patch series via email to a project that does not use GitHub or GitLab. The project maintainer accepts patches in `git format-patch` style.

Your repository is at `/home/user/patch-work`. It is a colocated jj+git repository. You have a stack of 5 commits above `main`:

1. `fix: correct off-by-one error in parser` — modifies `src/parser.py`
2. `fix: handle empty input in tokenizer` — modifies `src/tokenizer.py`
3. `refactor: extract validation helper` — creates `src/validation.py`
4. `test: add parser edge case tests` — modifies `tests/test_parser.py`
5. `docs: update parser documentation` — modifies `docs/parser.md`

The `patch-series` bookmark points to the tip (commit 5).

## Your Task

You need to export this 5-commit stack as a proper email patch series.

1. **Create the patches directory**: Create `/home/user/patches/` directory.

2. **Export each patch**: For each commit in the stack (commits 1-5 above `main`), create a patch file at:
   - `/home/user/patches/0001-fix-correct-off-by-one-error-in-parser.patch`
   - `/home/user/patches/0002-fix-handle-empty-input-in-tokenizer.patch`
   - `/home/user/patches/0003-refactor-extract-validation-helper.patch`
   - `/home/user/patches/0004-test-add-parser-edge-case-tests.patch`
   - `/home/user/patches/0005-docs-update-parser-documentation.patch`

   Each patch file must contain:
   - `From <COMMIT_HASH> Mon Sep 17 00:00:00 2001` header
   - `From: Contributor <contrib@example.com>`
   - `Date: <timestamp>`
   - `Subject: [PATCH N/5] <commit description>`
   - A blank line
   - The commit message body
   - `---`
   - The unified diff (the actual file changes)

   You can use `jj log` with templates and `jj diff` to generate these. Alternatively, since this is a colocated repo, you can use `git format-patch` after `jj git export`.

3. **Create a cover letter**: Write `/home/user/patches/0000-cover-letter.patch` with:
   - `From: Contributor <contrib@example.com>`
   - `Subject: [PATCH 0/5] Parser fixes and documentation update`
   - A blank line
   - `This series fixes two parser bugs, refactors validation logic, adds tests, and updates documentation.`
   - `---`
   - A list of the 5 patches with their subjects

4. **Write a log file**: Write `/home/user/patch_export_log.txt` with 5 lines, one per patch:
   ```
   0001: fix: correct off-by-one error in parser
   0002: fix: handle empty input in tokenizer
   0003: refactor: extract validation helper
   0004: test: add parser edge case tests
   0005: docs: update parser documentation
   ```

## Acceptance Criteria

- `/home/user/patches/` directory must exist with exactly 6 files (0000 through 0005).
- Each patch file (0001-0005) must contain `Subject: [PATCH` and the correct sequence number.
- Each patch file must contain a diff section (lines starting with `+` or `-`).
- The cover letter (0000) must contain `[PATCH 0/5]` in its Subject line.
- `/home/user/patch_export_log.txt` must exist with exactly 5 non-empty lines.
9:T128c,# Managing .gitignore and Untracking Files in jj

You are working through a **training scenario for command option tradeoffs** in `jj` (Jujutsu VCS). In this exercise, you will learn how `.gitignore` files interact with already-tracked files in a jj repository, and how to correctly stop tracking files that should be ignored.

---

## Background

`jj` uses `.gitignore` files to determine which files should not be automatically tracked. This works the same way as in Git — pattern rules in `.gitignore` tell jj which paths to ignore.

However, there is an important nuance: **files that are already being tracked will remain tracked even if they match an ignore pattern**. This means that adding an entry to `.gitignore` is not enough by itself to remove a file from the current working-copy commit. You must explicitly stop tracking such files using the `jj file untrack` command.

This is a common tripping point for developers migrating from Git, where `git rm --cached <file>` serves a similar purpose.

---

## Starting Environment

You start with:
- A jj repository at `/home/user/myrepo` (already initialised with `jj git init`)
- The jj user identity pre-configured (`user.name` and `user.email` are already set)
- Two files already tracked in the working-copy commit:
  - `/home/user/myrepo/src/main.py` — a source file that should remain tracked
  - `/home/user/myrepo/build/output.bin` — a build artifact that should NOT be tracked
- No `.gitignore` file exists yet in the repository

The current working-copy commit (`@`) already includes both files as tracked changes.

---

## Your Tasks

### Task 1: Create a .gitignore file

Create a `.gitignore` file at the root of the repository (`/home/user/myrepo/.gitignore`) that ignores the `build/` directory.

The file should contain the following single line:

```
build/
```

After creating this file, run `jj status` to observe the current state. You will notice that `build/output.bin` is still shown as a tracked file — merely creating `.gitignore` does not untrack already-tracked files.

**Hint:** You can create this file using standard shell tools like `echo` or a text editor.

### Task 2: Untrack the build artifact

Now that the ignore rule is in place, you need to explicitly stop tracking `build/output.bin`. Use the appropriate `jj` command to untrack this specific file.

Remember:
- The file must already be ignored (i.e., match a pattern in `.gitignore`) before you can untrack it
- You are untracking from the working-copy commit
- After untracking, `build/output.bin` should no longer appear in `jj status` output

**Important tradeoff to understand:** `jj file untrack` requires that the path is already ignored. If you try to untrack a file that is *not* covered by a `.gitignore` pattern, the command will fail. This is different from `git rm --cached`, which will remove tracking regardless of ignore rules.

**Hint:** Use the `jj file untrack` subcommand with the path to the file.

### Task 3: Verify the final state

After completing the above tasks, verify that:

1. Running `jj status` in `/home/user/myrepo` shows `src/main.py` as a tracked/modified file but does **not** show `build/output.bin`
2. The file `build/output.bin` still exists on disk at `/home/user/myrepo/build/output.bin` (untracking does not delete files)
3. The `.gitignore` file exists at `/home/user/myrepo/.gitignore` and contains the `build/` pattern
4. Running `jj file list` in the repository lists `src/main.py` and `.gitignore` but does **not** list `build/output.bin`

---

## Key Concepts

### Why doesn't adding .gitignore automatically untrack files?

In jj (and Git), ignore rules only prevent *new* automatic tracking of files. Files that were already part of a commit remain tracked until you explicitly remove them. The workflow is:

1. Add the ignore pattern to `.gitignore`
2. Explicitly untrack already-tracked files with `jj file untrack <paths>`

### Command option tradeoffs

- `jj file untrack <path>` — stops tracking a specific path; **requires** the path to be ignored first
- `jj restore --from <rev> <path>` — restores file content from another revision; does not change tracking
- `jj abandon` — abandons a revision entirely; not suitable for removing individual files from tracking

Understanding which command to use in each context is an important part of working effectively with jj.

---

## Expected Final State

- `/home/user/myrepo/.gitignore` exists and contains `build/` on its own line
- `build/output.bin` is present on disk but is no longer tracked in the working-copy commit
- `src/main.py` remains tracked
- `jj file list -r @` does not include `build/output.bin`
- `jj file list -r @` includes both `.gitignore` and `src/main.py`
a:T1178,# Initialize a Jujutsu Repository and Configure User Identity

You are a developer who has recently switched from Git to Jujutsu (jj). You have an existing project directory at `/home/user/myproject` that you want to bring under jj version control. Your goal is to initialise a jj repository in that directory and set your user identity in the global jj configuration so that commits are attributed to you.

## Background

Jujutsu (jj) is a modern version-control system with first-class support for Git backends. Unlike Git, jj separates the concept of a "change" (an intention to modify the codebase) from a "commit" (a snapshot of the repo at a point in time). Every working-copy state is automatically snapshotted, so there is no explicit staging area.

When you first use jj you must configure two pieces of identity information that will be embedded in every commit you author:

- **user.name** – your display name
- **user.email** – your e-mail address

These values live in the user-level (global) configuration file, not the repository-level file. On Linux the global config is stored at `~/.config/jj/config.toml` (or the path returned by `jj config path --user`).

## Starting Environment

- You are logged in as `user` with home directory `/home/user`.
- The directory `/home/user/myproject` already exists and contains a single file: `README.md` with the content `# My Project`.
- `jj` (version 0.38.0) is installed and available on `PATH` as `/usr/local/bin/jj`.
- No jj repository has been initialised yet anywhere under `/home/user`.
- No jj user-level configuration exists yet (i.e. `~/.config/jj/config.toml` does not exist).

## Your Tasks

### Task 1 — Initialise a jj repository

Initialise a jj repository **with a Git backend** inside `/home/user/myproject`.

The correct command to initialise a new Git-backed jj repo in an existing directory is:

```
jj git init /home/user/myproject
```

or, if you are already inside the directory:

```
cd /home/user/myproject
jj git init .
```

After running this command you should see a `.jj` subdirectory created inside `/home/user/myproject`.

**Verify:** Run `jj status` from inside `/home/user/myproject`. You should see output indicating that jj recognises the repository, for example:

```
The working copy is clean
Working copy : <change-id> <commit-id> (empty) (no description set)
Parent commit: zzzzzzzz 000000000000 (empty) (no description set)
```

### Task 2 — Set your user identity

Set the following values in the **user-level** (global) jj configuration:

| Key | Value |
|---|---|
| `user.name` | `Alex Developer` |
| `user.email` | `alex@example.com` |

Use `jj config set --user` for each key:

```
jj config set --user user.name "Alex Developer"
jj config set --user user.email "alex@example.com"
```

You can verify the values were saved correctly with:

```
jj config get user.name
jj config get user.email
```

Or list all user-level config settings:

```
jj config list --user
```

### Verification Conditions

The automated verifier will check **all** of the following:

1. The directory `/home/user/myproject/.jj` exists.
2. Running `jj status` inside `/home/user/myproject` exits with code 0.
3. Running `jj config get user.name` outputs exactly `Alex Developer`.
4. Running `jj config get user.email` outputs exactly `alex@example.com`.
5. Both `user.name` and `user.email` are set at the **user level** (not just repo level).
6. The file `/home/user/myproject/README.md` still exists and contains `# My Project`.

## Tips for Developers Migrating from Git

- `jj git init` in jj is analogous to `git init` in Git, but it creates a Git-compatible repository stored under `.jj/`.
- `jj config set --user` is analogous to `git config --global`. The `--user` flag means the setting is stored in your home directory's config file rather than in the repository.
- Unlike Git, jj does **not** require you to stage files before committing. The working copy is always automatically tracked.
- There is no concept of an "index" or "staging area" in jj.
- The command `jj status` is analogous to `git status`.

## Summary of Commands

```bash
# 1. Initialise jj repository with git backend
jj git init /home/user/myproject

# 2. Set user name globally
jj config set --user user.name "Alex Developer"

# 3. Set user email globally
jj config set --user user.email "alex@example.com"

# 4. Verify
cd /home/user/myproject
jj status
jj config get user.name
jj config get user.email
```

The repository is located at `/home/user/project`. It is a standard `jj`-backed Git repository. At some point during your colleague's session, an operation was performed that deleted or lost the `feature` bookmark and the commit it pointed to.

Before starting any recovery steps, take time to understand the current state of the repository:

- Note that the `feature` bookmark is **missing** from the current state.

## Constraints

- Use only `jj` commands (no `git` commands, no manual file editing).
- Do not use `sudo`.
- Do not run `jj op restore` — use `jj undo` instead.

d:T1409,# Recover Repo State Using `jj op restore`

You are an OS engineer evaluating how Jujutsu (jj) handles recovery from mistaken operations compared to Git's `git reflog`. The repository is at `/home/user/project`.

## Background

While experimenting with jj, you accidentally ran `jj squash` which merged the tip commit into its parent, losing the tip commit as a standalone revision. In Git you would use `git reflog` and `git reset --hard <sha>` to recover, but in jj the equivalent workflow uses the **operation log** (`jj op log`) and **`jj op restore`**.

Your goal is to understand the operation log, identify the operation that existed **before** the squash was run, and restore the repository to that earlier state — bringing back the lost commit as a standalone revision.

## Starting Environment

- The jj (git-backed) repository is at `/home/user/project`.
- The repo has **two visible commits** above the root:
  1. A commit with description **"add base module"** — adds the file `base.py`.
  2. A commit with description **"refactor: squash target"** — adds the file `refactor.py`. This commit was squashed into its parent in the most recent operation. Its changes are now absorbed into "add base module" and it no longer appears as a separate commit in `jj log`.
- The current working copy (`@`) is an **empty change** on top of the single merged commit.
- The file `refactor.py` is now part of the merged commit, not a separate standalone revision.

## Your Task

You must recover the original two-commit history by using jj's operation log. The workflow mirrors `git reflog` but is fully integrated into jj.

### Step 1: View the Operation Log

Run `jj op log` (alias for `jj operation log`) inside `/home/user/project` to see the history of operations performed on this repository. Each entry shows an operation ID, a timestamp, and a description of what was done.

Look at the operation descriptions. Identify the **most recent operation** (shown at the top) — it will describe a `squash` action. The operation **immediately below it** (its parent) represents the state of the repo **before** the squash happened.

### Step 2: Inspect the Pre-Squash State

Before restoring, verify you have found the correct operation by previewing what the repository looked like at that earlier point. Use:

```
jj --at-op=<OPERATION_ID> log
```

Replace `<OPERATION_ID>` with the ID of the operation that preceded the squash (you can use an unambiguous prefix — typically 8–12 hex characters). When the repo is loaded at that operation, you should see **two distinct commits** in the log: one with description **"add base module"** and one with description **"refactor: squash target"**.

This `--at-op` flag is jj's equivalent of `git show <reflog-sha>` — it lets you inspect the repo at any past operation without modifying anything.

### Step 3: Restore to the Pre-Squash Operation

Now restore the repository to the state at the operation you identified:

```
jj op restore <OPERATION_ID>
```

This command creates a **new operation** that sets the repo state back to what it was at the given operation ID. This is analogous to `git reset --hard <reflog-sha>` but safer — the squash operation is not deleted from the operation log; it is simply no longer the current state.

### Step 4: Verify the Recovery

After running `jj op restore`, confirm the recovery was successful:

1. Run `jj log` and verify **both** commits are now visible as separate revisions:
   - A commit with description **"add base module"**
   - A commit with description **"refactor: squash target"**
2. Check that the file `/home/user/project/refactor.py` exists on disk with content that includes the line `# refactor module`.
3. Run `jj op log` and confirm a new `restore` operation appears at the top of the operation log (the most recent operation).

## Key Differences from Git Reflog

- In Git: `git reflog` shows SHA history; recovery requires `git reset --hard <sha>` or `git checkout <sha>`.
- In jj: `jj op log` shows operation history with human-readable descriptions; recovery uses `jj op restore <op-id>` which is a **non-destructive forward operation** (it does not rewrite history — it adds a new operation on top).
- `jj op restore` is more precise than `jj undo` when you need to jump to a specific operation that is not the most recent one.

## Important Constraints

- Work exclusively with `jj` commands — do **not** use `git` commands.
- The working directory for all commands is `/home/user/project`.
- No root or sudo access is needed.
- Operation IDs are long hex strings; you only need an unambiguous prefix when passing them to `--at-op` or `op restore`.
- The `jj op restore` command accepts a single positional argument: the operation ID.

## Expected Final State

When you are done:
- `jj log` shows **two commits** above the root: **"add base module"** and **"refactor: squash target"**.
- `/home/user/project/refactor.py` exists on disk and contains `# refactor module`.
- `/home/user/project/base.py` exists on disk.
- `jj op log` shows a `restore` operation as the most recent entry at the top.

2. **`feat: add ingestion pipeline`** — creates `pipelines/ingest.py` containing `# ingest pipeline\n`
3. **`feat: add transformation step`** — creates `pipelines/transform.py` containing `# transform step\n`
4. **`draft: WIP validation logic (do not merge)`** — creates `pipelines/validate_wip.py` containing `# wip validation\n`
5. **`draft: experimental dedup pass`** — creates `pipelines/dedup_exp.py` containing `# experimental dedup\n`
6. **`feat: add reporting module`** — creates `reports/summary.py` containing `# reporting\n`

The bookmark `audit-main` currently points at revision **#6** (`feat: add reporting module`), which is the working-copy parent. The working copy (`@`) sits on an empty change on top of revision #6.

Revisions #4 and #5 (the two `draft:` revisions) are **stale drafts** that were merged into the main line before being cleaned up. Your task is to remove them from the history so the data team's audit trail is clean.

## Your Task

You must perform the following operations **inside** `/home/user/audit-repo`:

### Step 1 — Understand the current shape

Run `jj log` to confirm the current revision graph. You should see six non-root revisions plus the empty working-copy change. Take note of the change IDs of the two draft revisions whose descriptions start with `draft:`.

### Step 2 — Identify the draft revisions with a revset

Use `jj log -r` with a revset expression that matches **all revisions whose description starts with `draft:`**. The revset function you need is `description(glob:"draft:*")`. Confirm that exactly two revisions are matched.

### Step 3 — Abandon the draft revisions

Abandon **both** draft revisions in a single `jj abandon` command using the revset `description(glob:"draft:*")`. Because both drafts are in the middle of the chain, jj will automatically rebase their descendants (revisions #5, #6, and the working-copy change) to close the gaps.

1. `init: scaffold audit repo`
2. `feat: add ingestion pipeline`
3. `feat: add transformation step`
4. `feat: add reporting module`

### Step 4 — Move the `audit-main` bookmark

After the abandon + rebase, the `audit-main` bookmark may still be pointing to the old commit ID of revision #6. Move the bookmark so it points to the **parent of the current working-copy commit** (i.e., the rebased `feat: add reporting module` revision). Use `jj bookmark set audit-main -r @-` to do this.

### Step 5 — Verify no draft revisions remain

Run `jj log -r 'description(glob:"draft:*")'` and confirm the output is empty (no revisions matched). This is the key verification step.

### Step 6 — Confirm the file system reflects the cleaned history

Run `jj file list -r 'description("feat: add reporting module")'` and confirm that `reports/summary.py` is present. Also confirm that `pipelines/validate_wip.py` and `pipelines/dedup_exp.py` do **not** appear in the file listing for the current `audit-main` bookmark tip:


- `README.md`
- `pipelines/ingest.py`
- `pipelines/transform.py`
- `reports/summary.py`

The WIP and experimental files must not appear in the file listing at `audit-main`.

### Step 7 — Final log check

Run `jj log -r 'audit-main::@'` to confirm the bookmark and working copy relationship. The `audit-main` bookmark should be the direct parent of `@`.

## Constraints

- Work only inside `/home/user/audit-repo`.
- Do not use `--ignore-immutable`.
- Do not delete the `audit-main` bookmark; only move it.
- All operations must be `jj` commands only — no direct `git` commands.

11:T13b9,# Rebase a Feature Branch and Track the Rebased Bookmark

## Context

You are a platform engineer maintaining a monorepo stack for your team. Your repository has a `main` bookmark that serves as the integration branch. A colleague has been developing a new feature on a branch called `feature/add-metrics`, and the work consists of two commits stacked on top of an older base of `main`.

Since then, `main` has advanced with new infrastructure changes — specifically, a commit that adds a `deploy.sh` script. Your task is to rebase the entire `feature/add-metrics` branch on top of the updated `main` bookmark so the feature work incorporates the latest infrastructure, and then confirm that the `feature/add-metrics` bookmark is correctly tracking the rebased tip.

## Repository State

The working directory is `/home/user/monorepo`. The repository has already been initialized with `jj git init`. The commit history looks like this:

```
@ (working copy, empty)
│
o  feature-commit-2 — "feat: add metrics dashboard endpoint"
│                     [feature/add-metrics bookmark]
│
o  feature-commit-1 — "feat: scaffold metrics module"
│
o  old-main — "chore: initial project scaffold"
│             [main bookmark — OLD POSITION]
│
o  infra-update — "chore: add deploy.sh for CI pipeline"
│                 [main bookmark — CURRENT POSITION after update]
│
o  root commit
```

Wait — more precisely, the actual topology is:

- `root()` → commit A (`chore: initial project scaffold`) ← `main` was here
- commit A → commit B (`chore: add deploy.sh for CI pipeline`) ← `main` is now here  
- commit A → commit C (`feat: scaffold metrics module`) → commit D (`feat: add metrics dashboard endpoint`) ← `feature/add-metrics` bookmark

So `feature/add-metrics` was branched off commit A (old `main`), but `main` has since moved forward to commit B. The `feature/add-metrics` bookmark points at commit D.

## Files Present

- `/home/user/monorepo/` — the jj repository root
- `/home/user/monorepo/scaffold.sh` — added in the initial scaffold commit
- `/home/user/monorepo/deploy.sh` — added in the `main` update commit
- `/home/user/monorepo/metrics/module.py` — added by feature-commit-1
- `/home/user/monorepo/metrics/dashboard.py` — added by feature-commit-2

## Your Task

Perform the following steps in order:

### Step 1: Rebase the `feature/add-metrics` branch onto `main`

Use `jj rebase` with the `--source` (or `-s`) flag to rebase the root of the feature branch (commit C, the first feature commit) and its descendants onto the current `main`. This will replay both feature commits on top of the latest `main`.

```
jj rebase -s 'roots(main..feature/add-metrics)' -d main
```

This command says: find the root of the range between `main` and `feature/add-metrics`, and rebase that commit (along with its descendants) onto `main`.

### Step 2: Verify the rebased history

Inspect the log to confirm the feature branch is now on top of `main`:

```
jj log -r 'main::feature/add-metrics'
```

You should see two feature commits as descendants of `main`, with `main` as their ancestor.

### Step 3: Confirm the bookmark is tracking correctly

Verify that the `feature/add-metrics` bookmark has been automatically moved to the rebased tip:

```
jj bookmark list
```

You should see `feature/add-metrics` pointing to a commit whose parent is the commit pointed to by `main`.

## Expected Outcome

After completing this task:

1. **The `feature/add-metrics` bookmark** must point to a commit whose description is `feat: add metrics dashboard endpoint`.
2. **The parent of the `feature/add-metrics` tip** must be a commit whose description is `feat: scaffold metrics module`.
3. **The grandparent of the `feature/add-metrics` tip** must be the commit that `main` points to (description: `chore: add deploy.sh for CI pipeline`).
4. **Both files** `metrics/module.py` and `metrics/dashboard.py` must be present in the `feature/add-metrics` tip.
5. **The file** `deploy.sh` must also be present in the `feature/add-metrics` tip (inherited from `main`).

## Verification

To verify your work is complete, run:

```bash
cd /home/user/monorepo
jj log -r 'main::feature/add-metrics' --no-graph -T 'description ++ "\n"'
```

The output should list:
```
chore: add deploy.sh for CI pipeline
feat: scaffold metrics module
feat: add metrics dashboard endpoint
```

And:
```bash
jj file list -r 'feature/add-metrics'
```

Should include `deploy.sh`, `metrics/dashboard.py`, `metrics/module.py`, and `scaffold.sh`.

## Tips

- In jj, `jj rebase -s <revset> -d <destination>` rebases the given revision(s) and all their descendants onto the destination.
- The `roots(x..y)` revset finds the root(s) of the range — i.e., commits in `x..y` that are not descendants of other commits in `x..y`.
- After a rebase, bookmarks that pointed to the rebased commits are automatically moved to the corresponding new commits.
- You do not need to manually update the bookmark after rebase; jj handles this automatically.

Your monorepo has a `infra/base` bookmark that represents the stable shared infrastructure layer, and two feature branches — `services/auth-patch` and `services/cache-patch` — that your teammates have been working on.

The situation: both service patch branches were cut from an older version of `infra/base`. Since then, the infrastructure team has merged two new commits into `infra/base` — a commit that adds a shared `lib/config.py` configuration helper, and a commit that adds a `lib/health.py` health-check module. These new modules are required by both service patches, so both branches must be rebased onto the current `infra/base`.

Your job is to rebase each service patch branch onto the updated `infra/base`, verify that the bookmarks have been correctly moved to the rebased tips, and confirm that the new infrastructure files are visible in the rebased branches.

## Repository State

The working directory is `/home/user/monorepo`. The repository has already been initialized with `jj git init --colocate`. The initial commit graph looks like this:

```
root()
  └─ A: "chore: initial monorepo scaffold"  ← infra/base (OLD)
       ├─ B: "feat: add shared config helper"  (infra update 1)
       │    └─ C: "feat: add health check module"  ← infra/base (CURRENT)
       ├─ D: "fix: patch auth token validation"  ← services/auth-patch
       └─ E: "fix: add cache TTL enforcement"  ← services/cache-patch
```

In other words:
- Commit A is the old base of `infra/base` (the initial scaffold).
- Commits B and C are new infra commits; `infra/base` now points at commit C.
- Commits D and E are both children of commit A (old infra/base); they are the service patches.
- `services/auth-patch` points at commit D.
- `services/cache-patch` points at commit E.

## Files Present in the Starting Environment

- `/home/user/monorepo/` — the jj repository root (contains `.jj/` directory)
- `/home/user/monorepo/scaffold.py` — created in commit A ("chore: initial monorepo scaffold")
- `/home/user/monorepo/lib/config.py` — created in commit B ("feat: add shared config helper"), visible in `infra/base`
- `/home/user/monorepo/lib/health.py` — created in commit C ("feat: add health check module"), visible in `infra/base`
- `/home/user/monorepo/services/auth.py` — created in commit D, visible in `services/auth-patch`
- `/home/user/monorepo/services/cache.py` — created in commit E, visible in `services/cache-patch`

Note: The working copy is positioned at a new empty commit on top of `infra/base` (the `@` commit).

## Your Task

You must complete the following steps using only `jj` commands. Do **not** use `git` commands for history manipulation.

### Step 1: Inspect the current state

Before making any changes, understand the repository topology. Run:

```bash
jj log
```

and:

```bash
jj bookmark list
```

This will show you the three bookmarks (`infra/base`, `services/auth-patch`, `services/cache-patch`) and the diverged commit graph.

### Step 2: Rebase `services/auth-patch` onto `infra/base`

The `services/auth-patch` bookmark points at a single commit (D) that is a direct child of the old `infra/base` (commit A). You must move this commit so that it sits on top of the current `infra/base` (commit C).

Use `jj rebase` with the `--revision` (or `-r`) flag to rebase just this single commit (without bringing any descendants that don't exist here) onto `infra/base`:

```
jj rebase -r services/auth-patch -d infra/base
```

This rebases only the commit pointed to by `services/auth-patch` onto `infra/base`. Since jj automatically moves the bookmark to the rebased commit, `services/auth-patch` should now point to the rebased version of D.

### Step 3: Rebase `services/cache-patch` onto `infra/base`

Repeat the same operation for the `services/cache-patch` branch:

```
jj rebase -r services/cache-patch -d infra/base
```

After this, `services/cache-patch` should also point to a rebased commit whose parent is the current `infra/base`.

### Step 4: Verify bookmark positions

Run:

```bash
jj bookmark list
```

Confirm that:
- `infra/base` still points at commit C (description: `feat: add health check module`)
- `services/auth-patch` points at a commit whose description is `fix: patch auth token validation`
- `services/cache-patch` points at a commit whose description is `fix: add cache TTL enforcement`

### Step 5: Verify that the infrastructure files are visible in the rebased branches

Check that `lib/config.py` and `lib/health.py` are now present in both rebased service branches:

```bash
jj file list -r services/auth-patch
jj file list -r services/cache-patch
```

Both commands should include `lib/config.py` and `lib/health.py` in their output, proving that the rebased commits now see the new infrastructure files.

### Step 6: Verify the parent chain of each rebased branch

For each service branch, confirm that its direct parent is the `infra/base` commit:

```bash
jj log -r 'infra/base::services/auth-patch' --no-graph -T 'description ++ "\n"'
jj log -r 'infra/base::services/cache-patch' --no-graph -T 'description ++ "\n"'
```

The first command should output:
```
feat: add health check module
fix: patch auth token validation
```

The second command should output:
```
feat: add health check module
fix: add cache TTL enforcement
```

## Expected Outcome

After completing this task:

1. **`services/auth-patch` bookmark** must point at a commit with description `fix: patch auth token validation`.
2. **The parent of `services/auth-patch` tip** must be the commit pointed to by `infra/base` (description: `feat: add health check module`).
3. **`services/cache-patch` bookmark** must point at a commit with description `fix: add cache TTL enforcement`.
4. **The parent of `services/cache-patch` tip** must be the commit pointed to by `infra/base` (description: `feat: add health check module`).
5. **`lib/config.py` and `lib/health.py`** must be visible in both `services/auth-patch` and `services/cache-patch`.
6. **`services/auth.py`** must be visible in `services/auth-patch`.
7. **`services/cache.py`** must be visible in `services/cache-patch`.
8. **`infra/base`** must still point at the commit with description `feat: add health check module`.

## Tips

- `jj rebase -r <rev> -d <dest>` rebases a **single** revision (not its descendants) onto the destination. This is different from `-s`/`--source`, which also rebases descendants.
- Since commits D and E are leaf commits (no descendants), `-r` and `-s` behave identically here, but `-r` is more precise for single-commit rebases.
- After rebasing, jj automatically moves any bookmarks that pointed to the original commit to the new rebased commit. You do not need to manually run `jj bookmark set`.
- The `-` suffix in revsets (e.g. `services/auth-patch-`) means "parent of"; use this to inspect the parent of a bookmarked commit.
- Revset `x::y` means "all commits reachable from x to y" (inclusive of both endpoints if they are connected).
14:T13d3,# Recovering a Hidden (Abandoned) Commit in Jujutsu

## Background

As an OS engineer comparing `jj` to Git, you are exploring how Jujutsu handles abandoned commits that are no longer visible in the default log. In Git, abandoning a branch leaves commits effectively unreachable — they live as "dangling commits" in the object store, discoverable only via `git reflog` or `git fsck --unreachable`. In Jujutsu, the analogous concept is a **hidden** commit. When you run `jj abandon <rev>`, the commit is not deleted — it is hidden from the default log view but still stored. Unlike Git's reflog, Jujutsu provides a first-class **operation log** (`jj op log`) that records every mutation. Using the operation log, you can rediscover commits that were abandoned in earlier operations.

## Starting Environment

You have a jj repository at `/home/user/kernel-patch`. The repository has the following **visible** commit history:

```
@ (working copy)  - empty, no description
o  refactor: clean up interrupt handler  (contains file: irq.c)
o  init: add initial Makefile              (contains file: Makefile)
```

However, there is also a **hidden** commit in the repository. This commit was abandoned by a previous engineer who thought the work was no longer needed. The hidden commit:
- Has description: `feat: add watchdog timer support`
- Contains a file named `watchdog.c` with content `/* watchdog timer implementation */`
- Is a child of the root commit (it was on a separate line of history)

This hidden commit does **not** appear in `jj log` (the default visible history). Your mission is to find it and restore it.

## Your Task

Complete the following steps to recover the hidden commit.

### Step 1: Discover the hidden commit using the operation log

Jujutsu's operation log records every mutation to the repository. You can use the `at_operation()` revset function to look at what commits were visible at a previous operation. Run:

```bash
jj log -r 'at_operation(@-, all())'
```

This shows all commits (including now-hidden ones) that were visible at the immediately previous operation (`@-` means the operation before the current one). You should see the commit with description `feat: add watchdog timer support` with a `(hidden)` marker. Note its **commit ID** (the hex SHA shown on the right in the log).

Alternatively, you can look at the full operation log:

```bash
jj op log
```

And inspect a specific earlier state:

```bash
jj --at-op <op-id> log
```

### Step 2: Restore the hidden commit into visible history

Once you have the **commit ID** (not the change ID) of the hidden commit, use `jj new` to create a new visible commit whose parent is the hidden commit. This brings the hidden commit's ancestry into the visible graph.

First, get the commit ID:
```bash
jj log -r 'at_operation(@-, all())' --no-graph -T 'commit_id ++ "\n"'
```

Then use `jj new` with the commit ID to restore it:
```bash
jj new <commit-id-of-hidden-commit>
```

Note: For hidden commits, you must use the full **commit ID** (a hex SHA) rather than the change ID (the short alphanumeric identifier). The commit ID is visible in the `at_operation` log output.

After running this command, the hidden commit becomes visible in `jj log` because the new working-copy commit's parent chain now references it.

**Important**: After completing this step, running `jj log` (without extra flags) should show the commit with description `feat: add watchdog timer support` as a **visible** ancestor of your working copy.

### Step 3: Verify the recovery

Confirm that `watchdog.c` is accessible in the recovered commit:

```bash
jj file show watchdog.c -r 'description(substring:"watchdog")'
```

This should print:
```
/* watchdog timer implementation */
```

## Key jj Concepts Demonstrated

- **Hidden commits**: `jj abandon` makes commits hidden (not deleted)
- **Operation log** (`jj op log`): every jj mutation is recorded; unlike Git's reflog, it is first-class and structured
- **`at_operation(op, revset)`**: evaluates a revset against an earlier operation's state, surfacing commits that were visible then but are hidden now
- **`jj new <rev>`**: creates a new change on top of any commit, even hidden ones — making the hidden commit visible as an ancestor
- **`@-` in operation revset context**: the operation immediately before the current one

## Comparison to Git

| Concept | Git | jj |
|---------|-----|-----|
| Abandoned commits | Dangling objects (unreachable) | Hidden commits |
| How to see them | `git reflog`, `git fsck --unreachable` | `jj log -r 'at_operation(@-, all())'` |
| How to recover | `git checkout -b <branch> <sha>` | `jj new <commit-id>` |
| Lifecycle | ~30 days until gc | Until `jj util gc` is run |

## Success Criteria

You have successfully completed this task when:
1. Running `jj log` in `/home/user/kernel-patch` shows a commit with description `feat: add watchdog timer support` as a **visible** commit (no `(hidden)` marker)
2. The file `watchdog.c` exists in that visible commit and contains `/* watchdog timer implementation */`

The following are already in place:

  ```toml

  email = "dev@example.com"
  ```
- No aliases are pre-configured. No custom `ui.paginate` setting is configured.

## Your Tasks

You must complete the following two configuration steps using `jj` commands (specifically `jj config set --user`). Do **not** manually edit the config file with a text editor — use the CLI.

### Task 1: Define a command alias named `st`

Create a user-level `jj` alias named `st` that expands to the `jj status` command. When a user types `jj st` in any repository, it should behave exactly as if they typed `jj status`.

In `jj`, aliases are defined under the `[aliases]` section of the config file. Each alias maps a short name to a **list of strings** representing the command and its arguments. For example, an alias named `l` that expands to `jj log` would be stored as:

```toml
[aliases]
l = ["log"]
```

Your alias `st` should expand to the single-word command `status`. The config key path you need to set is `aliases.st`, and its value must be the TOML array `["status"]`.

**Important note on `jj config set` syntax:** When setting an array value with `jj config set`, you provide the value as a TOML expression. Arrays are written with square brackets, e.g., `'["status"]'`. Make sure to quote the entire value appropriately for your shell so that brackets and quotes are passed through correctly.

After setting this, verify it works by running `jj st` inside `/home/user/myrepo`. The output should match that of `jj status`.

### Task 2: Disable the pager for `jj` output

Configure `jj` so that it **never** paginates output, even when output is long. This is useful in training environments or CI contexts where a pager would block execution.

The relevant config key is `ui.paginate`. Its valid values are:
- `"auto"` — use pagination only when writing to a terminal (the default)
- `"never"` — always disable pagination

Set `ui.paginate` to `"never"` at the user level using `jj config set --user`.

After setting this, verify the setting by listing your configuration with `jj config list` and confirming that `ui.paginate` appears with value `"never"`.

## Verification Conditions

After completing both tasks, the following must be true:

1. **Alias verification:** The file `/home/user/.config/jj/config.toml` must contain an `[aliases]` section where `st` is set to the array `["status"]`. Specifically, the TOML key `aliases.st` must equal `["status"]`.

2. **UI pager verification:** The file `/home/user/.config/jj/config.toml` must contain a `[ui]` section (or a dotted key) where `ui.paginate` is set to the string value `"never"`.

3. **Functional alias test:** Running `jj st` inside `/home/user/myrepo` must succeed (exit code 0) and produce output consistent with `jj status` (i.e., it should not error with "unrecognized subcommand").

## Config File Location

The user-level config file is located at:
```
/home/user/.config/jj/config.toml
```

You can find its path programmatically with:
```
jj config path --user
```

## What NOT to Do

- Do **not** edit the config file with a text editor (`nano`, `vim`, etc.). Use `jj config set --user` commands only.
- Do **not** create a repo-level config (i.e., do not use `--repo` flag). All changes must be at `--user` level.
- Do **not** remove or modify the existing `[user]` section with `name` and `email`.

- `ui.paginate` → TOML value: `"never"`

Both settings must be present in `/home/user/.config/jj/config.toml` when you are done.

1a:T1391,# Preparing a Clean Release Branch with jj History Rewriting

## Scenario

You are a release manager at a software company preparing the `v2.3` release of the `widget-engine` project. The repository lives at `/home/user/widget-engine`. A developer has been working on the `release/v2.3` bookmark and the history is messy — it needs to be cleaned up before the release can be tagged.

Run `jj log -r 'root()..@'` in the `/home/user/widget-engine` directory to inspect the current history. You will find the following chain of commits on the `release/v2.3` bookmark (from oldest to newest):

1. **"initial project scaffold"** — the base commit (this is correct, do not modify it)
2. **"add API endpoint and fix login bug"** — this commit **incorrectly bundles** two unrelated changes together:
   - Changes to `src/api.py` (the new API endpoint)
   - Changes to `src/auth.py` (the login bug fix)
3. **"add release notes"** — adds the file `RELEASE_NOTES.md` (this is correct, do not modify it)
4. **"wip: bump version"** — adds/modifies `version.txt` with the new version string (the description is wrong — it should be `"bump version to 2.3.0"`)
5. **"fixup: missing newline in release notes"** — a small fixup commit that modifies `RELEASE_NOTES.md` (this needs to be squashed into the "add release notes" commit)

The `release/v2.3` bookmark currently points to commit 5 (the topmost commit).

## Your Tasks

Perform the following operations **in order**, all within the `/home/user/widget-engine` jj repository:

### Task 1 — Split the mixed commit

The commit with description **"add API endpoint and fix login bug"** contains changes to two unrelated files: `src/api.py` and `src/auth.py`. You must split it into **two separate commits** with the following descriptions (in order, oldest first):

- First commit (parent): description must be exactly `"fix login bug"` — contains **only** the changes to `src/auth.py`
- Second commit (child): description must be exactly `"add API endpoint"` — contains **only** the changes to `src/api.py`

Use `jj split` with the file argument to split by file path without entering an interactive diff editor. Pass `-m` to set the description of the first (selected) commit. After splitting, use `jj describe` to rename the second commit from its inherited description to `"add API endpoint"`.

**Important**: `jj split` by default puts the **selected files** (those you pass as arguments) into the **first** commit and the remaining files into the second. So think carefully about which file to select.

### Task 2 — Squash the fixup commit

The commit with description **"fixup: missing newline in release notes"** (which modifies `RELEASE_NOTES.md`) is a small fixup that logically belongs in the **"add release notes"** commit. Squash it into the "add release notes" commit using `jj squash`.

After the squash, the commit with description "add release notes" should contain all changes to `RELEASE_NOTES.md`. The "fixup: missing newline in release notes" commit must no longer exist in the history.

Use `jj squash --from <rev> --into <rev>` to move the changes from the fixup commit into the "add release notes" commit. You will need to identify the correct revision specifiers using `jj log`. Use `--use-destination-message` to keep the destination commit description.

## Context

The repository has two commits that both describe themselves as `fix: patch` but have different content in `src/patch.py`. ## Repository State

Use `jj log -r 'description("fix: patch")'` to find the two divergent commits. Both are children of `main` and have:
- **Commit A (patch-v1)**: `src/patch.py` with function `def apply_patch_v1()` and the `patch` bookmark pointing to it
- **Commit B (patch-v2)**: `src/patch.py` with function `def apply_patch_v2()` and no bookmark

## Your Task

### Step 1: Identify the divergent commits

Run:
```
jj log -r 'description("fix: patch")'
```
Note the two change IDs. Examine each one's content.

### Step 2: Reconcile by squashing into a single canonical commit

Use `jj new` to create a merge of both divergent commits, then squash into one:

Option A — Merge then squash:
```
jj new <patch-v1-rev> <patch-v2-rev> -m "fix: patch"
```
Then edit `src/patch.py` to contain BOTH functions:
```python
def apply_patch_v1():
    """Apply patch version 1."""
    return "patch_v1"


def apply_patch_v2():
    """Apply patch version 2."""
    return "patch_v2"


def apply_patch():
    """Apply the canonical patch (combines v1 and v2)."""
    return apply_patch_v1(), apply_patch_v2()
```

### Step 3: Verify single canonical commit

After reconciliation, verify that `jj log -r 'description(exact:"fix: patch")'` returns exactly ONE commit (the reconciled one).

### Step 4: Update the `patch` bookmark

Move the `patch` bookmark to the canonical commit:
```
jj bookmark move patch --to @
```

### Step 5: Write the divergence log

Write `/home/user/divergence_log.txt` with exactly this format:
```
approach: merge_then_edit
patch_v1_change_id: <change_id_of_original_patch_v1>
patch_v2_change_id: <change_id_of_original_patch_v2>
canonical_change_id: <change_id_of_reconciled_commit>
resolution: both functions combined in single commit
```

## Constraints

- The final `src/patch.py` must contain both `def apply_patch_v1()` and `def apply_patch_v2()`
- The `patch` bookmark must exist and point to the reconciled commit
- The log file must use the exact format shown
- No conflict markers in `src/patch.py`
